19:754:55912:18:18:68:434:292:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:this restricted sample; the results are virtually unchanged (Figure 5b)).
19:755:55985:18:18:85:543:274:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:In order to assure that the differences in the hazard rates are in fact due to the reform in
19:756:56077:18:17:68:543:256:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the UI system and not simply the result of some general trend, we exploit the fact that we have
19:757:56172:17:18:68:543:239:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:additional data from 2004 and after 2006. First, we estimate two placebo tests for whether
19:758:56262:18:18:68:543:221:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:there are differences in the two years before the reform and the one year before the reform,
19:759:56354:18:18:68:543:203:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:using the same estimation strategy as before. We report these results in Appendix Figure A-
19:760:56445:18:18:68:543:185:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:3a), revealing that the hazard rates are virtually unchanged between 2004 and 2005. There is
19:761:56537:18:18:68:543:167:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:a small difference right after the 270 line, which is expected due to the reduction in unemploy-
19:762:56633:18:18:68:543:149:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:ment assistance in February 2005, leading to a slight increase in the hazard at this point in
19:763:56726:18:18:68:543:131:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:2005. Similarly Appendix Figure A-3b) shows that there are virtually no differences between
19:764:56817:18:18:68:543:113:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the hazards 1 and 2 years after the reform, again indicating that the differences between our
19:765:56910:18:15:77:543:95:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:F:23Alternatively we also used propensity score reweighting to estimate the hazards in the pre- and post-period,
19:766:57020:15:0:68:477:80:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:holding the observables constant over time and obtained almost identical results (not shown).
20:767:57115:0:18:68:350:711:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:before- and after-period line up nicely with the reform.
20:768:57171:18:18:85:543:693:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:We explore the timing further by plotting time-series graphs of the exit hazards over
20:769:57256:18:18:68:543:675:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:specific intervals. Figure 6a) shows the evolution over time of the exit hazard between 30
20:770:57346:18:18:68:543:657:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:and 90 days (red line) and between 90 and 150 days (black line).  Each dot indicates the
20:771:57434:18:17:68:543:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:average hazard for each 3-month period between 2004 and 2007, with quarter 1 indicating
20:772:57521:17:18:68:543:622:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the first 3-month period after the reform. Prior to the reform, the hazard at 90-150 days is
20:773:57613:18:18:68:543:604:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:smaller than the hazard at 30-90 days, consistent with the patterns in Figure 5. Subsequent
20:774:57704:18:18:68:543:586:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:to the reform introducing a step down of benefits after 90 days, the pattern abruptly changes.
20:775:57798:18:18:68:543:568:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Already in the first quarter after the reform, the hazard at 90-150 days increases sizeably,
20:776:57890:18:18:68:543:550:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:becoming similar to the hazard at 30-90 days, a pattern that remains largely similar over the
20:777:57983:18:18:68:543:532:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:next 6 quarters. The figure provides little evidence of previous trends, suggesting that the
20:778:58075:18:18:68:370:514:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:changes in hazards are indeed a causal effect of the reform.
20:779:58135:18:18:85:543:496:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Figure 6b) provides parallel evidence for the hazard at 210-270 days versus at 270-330
20:780:58221:18:18:68:543:478:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:days. In the quarters pre-reform, the hazard at 270-330 days is significantly higher than the
20:781:58314:18:18:68:543:460:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:hazard at 210-270 days, a pattern that changes abruptly with the first quarter following the
20:782:58406:18:18:68:543:442:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:reform. The time-series plots again indicate a change that is coincidental with the reform and
20:783:58500:18:36:68:445:424:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:not due underlying trends or changes in the macroeconomic environment.
20:784:58570:36:29:68:217:388:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:C:5  Structural Estimation
20:785:58594:29:26:68:231:359:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:C:5.1  Set-up and Estimation
20:786:58620:26:18:68:543:333:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:We use the model of Section 2, imposing six additional assumptions, some of which we relax
20:787:58710:18:18:68:543:315:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:later. The first three assumptions concern the utility function. First, we assume that the
20:788:58800:18:18:68:543:297:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f14:B:search cost function has a power form as in Paserman (2008) and Chetty (2003):c(s) =
20:789:58884:18:18:68:543:279:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f5:B:ks1+γ/(1 +γ).  This form implies that the parameterγis the inverse of the elasticity of
20:790:58971:18:18:68:543:261:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:search effort with respect to the net value of employment.24Second, we assume log utility,
20:791:59061:18:18:68:543:243:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f5:B:v(b) = ln (b). Third, similar to Bloemen (2005) and Paserman (2008), we model heterogeneity
20:792:59152:18:18:68:443:225:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:as three types of unemployed workers that differ in their cost of searchk.
20:793:59226:18:17:85:543:207:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Fourth, to avoid modelling on-the-job search, we start the worker problem in the first
20:794:59312:17:18:68:543:190:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:period of unemployment, and thus fit the hazard from the second period on.25Fifth, we set
20:795:59401:18:18:68:543:172:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:past wages equal to the median earnings in our sample, which is 135,000 HUF ($675), and
20:796:59488:18:18:68:542:154:12:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f9:B:assume that reemployment wages are constant over the UI spell and equal to past wages.26
20:797:59576:18:16:77:543:136:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:F:24To see this, recall that the first-order condition of search effort (equation 4) isc′(s∗) =v,where we
20:798:59679:16:13:68:543:120:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:denote withvthe net value of employment (that is, the right-hand-side of equation 4). Given the parametric
20:799:59785:13:9:68:537:107:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f19:F:assumption, this yieldss∗= (v/k)1/γ,and the elasticity ofs∗with respect tovisηs,v= (ds/dv)v/s= 1/γ.
20:800:59884:9:12:77:407:98:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:A:25Recall that a successful job search in periodtyields a job in periodt+ 1.
20:801:59959:12:0:77:445:86:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:A:26In a robustness check, we present estimates assuming a lower reemployment wage.
21:802:60042:0:18:68:543:711:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Sixth, we assume that individuals start with zero assets, that they cannot borrow against
21:803:60131:18:18:68:413:693:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:their future income, and that they earn no interest on saved assets.
21:804:60199:18:18:85:544:675:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:The vector of parametersξthat we estimate for the standard model are: (i) the three
21:805:60282:18:18:68:543:657:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:levels of search costkhigh, kmed,andklow,with the assumptionkhigh≥kmed≥klow,and
21:806:60361:18:17:68:543:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the two probability weightsplowandpmed;(ii) the search cost curvatureγ; (iii) the time
21:807:60447:17:18:68:543:622:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:preference parametersδandβ.For the reference-dependent model, we estimate in addition:
21:808:60533:18:18:68:543:604:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:(iv) the loss aversion parameterλ; and (v) the number of (15-day) periodsNover which the
21:809:60621:18:18:68:543:586:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:backward-looking reference point is formed.27To keep the number of parameters the same
21:810:60707:18:18:68:543:568:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:as for the standard model, in the reference-dependent model we assume only two cost types,
21:811:60797:18:18:68:543:550:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:thus removing parameterskmedandpmed. Notice that the weightηon gain-loss utility is set to
21:812:60887:18:18:68:543:532:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:1 in the benchmark estimates rather than being estimated; thus, the loss-aversion parameter
21:813:60978:18:18:68:543:514:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f5:B:λcan be interpreted also as the overall weight on the losses. The reason for this assumption
21:814:61070:18:18:68:543:496:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:is that over the course of the unemployment spell the individual is always on the loss side
21:815:61161:18:18:68:543:478:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:since the benefits are always (weakly) lower than the reference point. Hence, it is difficult to
21:816:61257:18:18:68:371:460:12:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f9:B:estimate a separate weight on gain utility and loss utility.28
21:817:61319:18:18:85:543:442:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f5:A:Estimation.We use a minimum-distance estimator.  Denote bym(ξ)the vector of
21:818:61394:18:18:68:543:424:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:moments predicted by the theory as a function of the parametersξ, and byˆmthe vector of
21:819:61481:18:18:68:543:406:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:observed moments. The minimum-distance estimator chooses the parametersˆξthat minimize
21:820:61567:18:17:68:543:388:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the distance(m(ξ)−ˆm)′W(m(ξ)−ˆm),whereWis a weighting matrix.  As a weighting
21:821:61644:17:18:68:543:371:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:matrix, we use a diagonal matrix that has as diagonal elements the inverse of the variance of
21:822:61737:18:18:68:543:353:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:each moment.29To calculate the theoretical moments, we use backward induction. First we
21:823:61824:18:18:68:543:335:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:numerically compute the steady-state search and steady-state value of unemployment using
21:824:61912:18:18:68:543:317:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:a hybrid bisection-quadratic interpolation method, pre-implemented in Matlab as the fzero
21:825:62001:18:18:68:543:299:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:routine. Then we solve backwards for the optimal search intensity and consumption path in
21:826:62090:18:18:68:543:281:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:each period as a function of the asset level. Finally, we use the initial asset level as a starting
21:827:62189:18:18:68:501:263:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:value to determine the actual consumption path and search intensity in each period.
21:828:62272:18:18:85:541:245:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f6:A:Under standard conditions, the minimum-distance estimator using weighting matrixW
21:829:62353:18:18:68:543:227:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:achieves asymptotic normality, with estimated variance(ˆG′WˆG)−1(ˆG′WˆΛWˆG)(ˆG′WˆG)−1/N,
21:830:62441:18:0:68:148:209:12:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f7:B:whereˆG≡N−1
21:831:62452:0:11:151:168:217:10:g_d0_f18:8:g_d0_f12:?:∑N
21:832:62454:11:15:161:544:206:8:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f14:F:i=1∇ξmi(ˆξ)andˆΛ≡V ar[m(ˆξ)](Wooldridge, 2010). We calculate∇ξm(ˆξ)
21:833:62521:15:18:68:415:191:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:numerically in Matlab using an adaptive finite difference algorithm.
21:834:62589:18:18:85:543:173:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f5:A:Moments.As momentsm(ξ)we use the 15-day hazard rates from day 15 to day 540 for
21:835:62668:18:18:68:543:155:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:both the pre-reform and post-reform period, for a total of 35*2=70 moments. We do not use
21:836:62757:18:17:68:534:137:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the hazard from the first 15 day period, since it would require modelling search on the job.
21:837:62849:17:12:77:470:120:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:A:27In the estimations tables we report the speed of adjustment in days, which is just N*15.
21:838:62939:12:16:77:543:108:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:F:28In principle, the weight on gain utilityηcan be separately identified as we show in a robustness section,
21:839:63046:16:12:68:543:92:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:since gain utility affects the utility of reemployment, but the reemployment utility does not allow for very
21:840:63154:12:8:68:180:80:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:precise identification ofη.
21:841:63181:8:0:77:481:72:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:A:29As robustness check below, we alternatively use the identity matrix as a weighting matrix.
22:842:63275:0:18:85:543:711:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f5:A:Identification.While the parameters are identified jointly, it is possible to address the
22:843:63364:18:18:68:543:693:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:main sources of identification of individual parameters. The cost of effort parameterskjare
22:844:63455:18:18:68:543:675:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:identified from both the level of search intensity and the path of the hazards over time. This
22:845:63549:18:18:68:543:657:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:is clearest in the standard model, where the heterogeneity in the parameters is needed, for
22:846:63640:18:17:68:543:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:example, to explain the decay in the hazard after day 360, when benefits remain constant and
22:847:63732:17:18:68:543:622:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:thus, in absence of heterogeneity, the hazard would be constant in the standard model (but
22:848:63822:18:18:68:543:604:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:not in the reference-dependent model). The search cost curvature parameter,γ,is identified
22:849:63912:18:18:68:544:586:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:by the responsiveness of the hazard rate to changes in benefits since1/γis the elasticity of
22:850:64004:18:18:68:376:568:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:search effort with respect to the (net) value of finding a job.
22:851:64067:18:18:85:543:550:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:The time preference parameters are identified by the presence of spikes around benefit
22:852:64153:18:18:68:543:532:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:cuts, among other moments. If the unemployed workers are patient, they save in advance
22:853:64239:18:18:68:543:514:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:of benefit decreases so as to smooth consumption. Impatient workers, instead, save little if
22:854:64331:18:18:68:543:496:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:at all and thus experience a sharp decrease in consumption around the benefit change. This
22:855:64421:18:18:68:522:478:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:consumption drop then induces a sharp increase in search effort as the benefits decrease.
22:856:64510:18:18:85:543:460:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Turning to the reference-dependence parameters, for a given value ofη(fixed to 1 in the
22:857:64597:18:18:68:543:442:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:benchmark specification),λdenotes the magnitude of the loss utility. A major component to
22:858:64686:18:18:68:543:424:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:identification is the extent to which the hazard for the pre-period is higher both before and
22:859:64779:18:18:68:543:406:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:after day 270, in response to a larger loss. Instead, the standard model has essentially identical
22:860:64877:18:17:68:543:388:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:hazards from day 270 onwards. The loss parameter is also identified by the response to other
22:861:64969:17:18:68:543:371:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:changes in the benefits, such as at 90 days in the post-period.  The parameterN, which
22:862:65055:18:18:68:543:353:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:indicates the speed at which the losses are reabsorbed into the reference point, is identified
22:863:65149:18:18:68:543:335:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:by the fact that the pre- and post-reform hazards converge a few months after day 270. The
22:864:65239:18:35:68:538:317:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:speed of convergence of the hazard after day 90 also suggests several months of adjustment.
22:865:65330:35:26:68:253:282:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:C:5.2  Hand-to-Mouth Estimates
22:866:65358:26:17:68:543:256:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:To build intuition, we first present estimates assuming that workers in each period consume
22:867:65449:17:18:68:543:239:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the per-period income. Figure 7a) presents the fit for the standard model with 3-type hetero-
22:868:65542:18:18:68:543:221:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:geneity. The model fits quite well the surge in hazard around day 90 in the post-period, and
22:869:65634:18:18:68:543:203:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the decreasing path of the hazard in the first 200 days. The fit is also reasonably good for
22:870:65726:18:18:68:543:185:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the period from day 400 on. However, the fit between days 250 and 400 is poor. As discussed
22:871:65817:18:18:68:543:167:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:above, the standard model predicts that the hazard rates for the pre- and post-period should
22:872:65909:18:18:68:543:149:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:be almost exactly the same after day 270. As such, the model misses both the sharp difference
22:873:66002:18:18:68:519:131:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:in hazard between day 260 and day 360, as well as the spikes at both 260 and 360 days.
22:874:66088:18:18:85:543:113:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:In comparison, Figure 7b) displays the fit of the reference-dependent model with two types
22:875:66178:18:18:68:543:95:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:(and thus the same number of parameters as in the standard model). The fit in the first 250
22:876:66269:18:0:68:543:77:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:days is very good, though it was quite good also for the standard model. But, as anticipated,
23:877:66364:0:18:68:543:711:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the model does much better for longer durations, where the standard model fits poorly. In
23:878:66453:18:18:68:543:693:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:particular, the model fits better the surge in the hazard rate in the pre-period in anticipation
23:879:66549:18:18:68:543:675:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:of the benefit cut after 270 days (which is larger in the pre period than in the post-period), as
23:880:66646:18:18:68:543:657:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:well as the elevated level for the following three months, compared to the pre-period. Then
23:881:66737:18:17:68:543:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the model tracks quite well the period following the exhaustion of unemployment assistance
23:882:66827:17:18:68:543:622:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:(after 360 days). The fit of the reference-dependent model, while superior to the standard
23:883:66917:18:18:68:543:604:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:model, is certainly not perfect. The most striking aspect of the data which the model does
23:884:67007:18:18:68:543:586:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:not capture is the large spike on day 270 for the pre-period; storable offers may play a role
23:885:67100:18:18:68:543:568:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:in this case. In addition, the reference-dependent model under-fits the difference in hazards
23:886:67193:18:18:68:313:550:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:between the pre- and post-period after day 270.
23:887:67240:18:18:85:543:532:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:In Table 2 we present the parameter estimates. The estimates for the standard model (Col-
23:888:67329:18:18:68:543:514:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:umn (1)) indicate substantial heterogeneity in cost k and low cost curvatureˆγ=.13. This
23:889:67417:18:18:68:543:496:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:implies a high elasticity of search effort to incentives, needed to fit the substantial hazard
23:890:67511:18:18:68:543:478:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:increases in response to benefits changes. The estimates for the reference-dependent model
23:891:67601:18:18:68:543:460:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:(Column (2)) indicate a substantial weight on loss utility,ˆλ= 3.5(s.e. 1.3), and slow adjust-
23:892:67695:18:18:68:543:442:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:ment of the reference point,ˆN= 216(s.e. 9) days. At the bottom of the Table, we report the
23:893:67786:18:18:68:543:424:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:goodness of fit (GOF) measure(m(ξ)−ˆm)′W(m(ξ)−ˆm). The reference-dependent model
23:894:67866:18:34:68:516:406:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:has a substantially better fit (GOF of 170 versus 215), for equal number of parameters.
23:895:67953:34:26:68:389:372:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:C:5.3  Benchmark Estimates with Consumption-Savings
23:896:68002:26:18:68:543:346:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:While the previous estimates indicate that a reference-dependent model can fit the patterns in
23:897:68096:18:18:68:543:328:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the data quite well, there is a key concern with the hand-to-mouth estimates. Individuals who
23:898:68189:18:18:68:543:310:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:anticipate experiencing loss utility from a benefit cut should save in anticipation, allowing
23:899:68282:18:18:68:543:292:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:them to smooth consumption around the benefit cut. In turn, this would imply smoother
23:900:68367:18:18:68:543:274:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:hazards around the benefit cuts than in the data. This consumption smoothing, however, is
23:901:68456:18:17:68:267:256:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:ruled out in the hand-to-mouth model.
23:902:68493:17:18:85:543:239:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:A parallel issue arises for the hand-to-mouth estimates of the standard model. The esti-
23:903:68581:18:18:68:543:221:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:mated patience parameter in the standard model (ˆδ=.98) implies forward-looking individuals
23:904:68672:18:18:68:543:203:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:who are quite responsive in their search effort to future benefit cuts. And yet, by assumption
23:905:68766:18:18:68:543:185:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:these individuals do not save in anticipation of the cuts. For these reasons, we now turn to
23:906:68858:18:18:68:543:167:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the estimates modelling a consumption-savings choice, in addition to the search effort choice.
23:907:68952:18:18:85:543:149:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Figure 8a) shows the fit for the standard model with three types, compared with the fit in
23:908:69042:18:18:68:543:131:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Figure 8b) for a reference-dependent model with two types (with once again the same number
23:909:69132:18:18:68:543:113:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:of parameters). The qualitative fit is similar to the fit obtained for hand-to-mouth consumers
23:910:69226:18:18:68:515:95:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:(Figures 7a) and b)): the reference-dependent model better fits the path in the hazard.
23:911:69313:18:0:85:543:77:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:How is that possible in light of the above intuition about counteracting savings? For an
24:912:69403:0:18:68:543:711:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:answer, we turn to Table 3 which displays the corresponding parameter estimates in Columns
24:913:69493:18:18:68:543:693:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:(1) and (2). The most striking element is the high estimated degree of impatience: 15-day
24:914:69582:18:18:68:543:675:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:discount factors ofδ= 0.89for the reference-dependent model andδ= 0.93for the standard
24:915:69668:18:18:68:543:657:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:model. Either estimate implies an annual discount factor of 0.17 or lower. Appendix Figure
24:916:69758:18:17:68:543:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:A-4a) provides further evidence on the identification of the discount factor. Each point in
24:917:69849:17:18:68:543:622:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the figure indicates the goodness of fit of the best-fitting estimate for a particular (15-day)
24:918:69944:18:18:68:543:604:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:discount factor. For patient individuals (δ= 0.995or higher), the reference-dependent model
24:919:70035:18:18:68:543:586:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:does poorly: loss-averse workers with a high degree of patience would build a buffer stock,
24:920:70126:18:18:68:543:568:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f14:B:thus smoothing the loss utility. As individuals become more impatient, already forδ= 0.95
24:921:70215:18:18:68:543:550:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the reference dependent model has a good fit (and better than the standard model), with the
24:922:70306:18:18:68:543:532:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:best fit, as we saw in the table, for an even lower discount factor. The fit of the standard
24:923:70398:18:18:68:443:514:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:model also improves as the discount factor decreases, though less steeply.
24:924:70472:18:18:85:543:496:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:These results stress the importance of modelling optimal consumption: both the standard
24:925:70559:18:18:68:543:478:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:model and the reference-dependent model have a somewhat worse fit compared to the hand-to-
24:926:70649:18:18:68:543:460:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:mouth estimates. The hand-to-mouth results embed an inconsistent combination of relatively
24:927:70739:18:18:68:543:442:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:high patience (and thus forward-looking search effort) and yet no consumption smoothing. In
24:928:70830:18:18:68:543:424:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the consumption-savings model, the discount factor drives at the same time both the search
24:929:70920:18:18:68:540:406:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:decision and the consumption-savings decision, putting additional restrictions on the model.
24:930:71012:18:17:85:543:388:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Returning to the point estimates, the downside of this set of results is that the model which
24:931:71105:17:18:68:543:371:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:best accommodates the data – the reference-dependent one – requires a degree of impatience
24:932:71195:18:18:68:543:353:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:which is hard to reconcile with other estimates in the literature. Yet, this high estimated
24:933:71286:18:18:68:543:335:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:degree of impatience may be due to a mis-specification of the discounting function. A growing
24:934:71379:18:18:68:543:317:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:body of evidence, summarized among others in Frederick et al. (2002) and DellaVigna (2009),
24:935:71470:18:18:68:543:299:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:suggests that the beta-delta model of time preferences due to Laibson (1997) and O’Donoghue
24:936:71561:18:18:68:543:281:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:and Rabin (1999) provides a better fit of observed behavior in a number of settings. The beta-
24:937:71655:18:18:68:543:263:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:delta model includes an additional discount factorβbetween the present and the next period
24:938:71745:18:18:68:366:245:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:to capture the present bias, inducing a time inconsistency.
24:939:71804:18:18:85:543:227:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Thus, in a second set of estimates we allow for beta-delta discounting30in both the stan-
24:940:71893:18:18:68:543:209:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:dard model and the reference-dependent model. To keep the number of parameters constant,
24:941:71981:18:18:68:543:191:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:we set the long-term discount factorδto .995. The results in Columns (3) and (4) of Table 3
24:942:72072:18:18:68:543:173:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:and in Figure 8c) show that the fit is better than in the models with delta discounting for the
24:943:72167:18:18:68:543:155:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:reference-dependent model with much more plausible discounting: the estimated present-bias
24:944:72257:18:17:68:544:137:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:parameter isβ= 0.58,implying a discount factor of 0.46 for the first year and of 0.88 for
24:945:72346:17:18:68:543:120:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:subsequent years.  This indicates a substantial degree of impatience, but in line with esti-
24:946:72438:18:16:77:543:102:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:F:30We assume that consumers are naive about the future self-control problem mostly for computational
24:947:72537:16:12:68:543:86:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:simplicity, especially given the complexity of estimating the consumption decision. In addition, the naiveté
24:948:72645:12:0:68:404:74:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:assumption is arguably better supported by the evidence (DellaVigna, 2009).
25:949:72722:0:18:68:543:711:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:mates in the literature. For example, Paserman (2008), building on the model in DellaVigna
25:950:72812:18:18:68:543:693:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:and Paserman (2005), estimates a job search model with beta-delta preferences and obtains
25:951:72901:18:18:68:543:675:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:estimates for beta ranging between 0.40 and 0.89, depending on the sample. Laibson et al.
25:952:72990:18:18:68:506:657:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:(2007), based on life-cycle consumption choices, estimates aβbetween 0.51 and 0.82.
25:953:73073:18:17:85:543:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:In light of both the higher plausibility and the better fit, we adopt the reference-dependent
25:954:73166:17:18:68:543:622:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:model with beta-delta discounting as the benchmark behavioral model in the rest of the paper.
25:955:73259:18:18:68:543:604:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:For the standard model, especially given the small difference in fit between the two discounting
25:956:73355:18:18:68:356:586:12:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f9:B:functions, we use the more standard delta discounting.31
25:957:73411:18:18:85:543:568:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:How do the two models achieve their fit? In Appendix Figures A-5 and A-6 we report plots
25:958:73499:18:18:68:543:550:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:for key model components, focusing on the high-cost type. In the standard model, the flow
25:959:73588:18:18:68:543:532:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:utility follows the step down in the benefits, with the size of the later steps accentuated by the
25:960:73686:18:18:68:543:514:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:curvature of the utility function. In the reference-dependent model, the flow utility captures
25:961:73780:18:18:68:543:496:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:also the intensity of the loss relative to the reference point.  The value of unemployment
25:962:73870:18:18:68:543:478:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:decreases over time in the standard model as expected, while in the reference-dependent
25:963:73957:18:18:68:543:460:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:model it actually increases over most of the range, reflecting the importance of reference
25:964:74047:18:18:68:543:442:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:point adaptation. This fits the observed decrease in search effort over time, even for a given
25:965:74141:18:18:68:543:424:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:type.  Furthermore, the value of unemployment declines sharply in correspondence to the
25:966:74228:18:18:68:543:406:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:benefit drop. (This sharp drop reflects the estimated impatience). The next panel shows the
25:967:74319:18:17:68:543:388:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:reference point path, which is decreasing over time. Notice that from around day 250 the
25:968:74407:17:18:68:543:371:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:reference point is higher in the pre-reform group, which contributes to generate higher loss
25:969:74499:18:18:68:438:353:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:utility and thus a larger increase in search effort near benefit expiration.
25:970:74575:18:18:85:543:335:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:In Appendix Figure A-6, the value of employment, which is almost constant in the standard
25:971:74664:18:18:68:543:317:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:model, increases monotonically over time for the reference-dependent model, as getting a job
25:972:74756:18:18:68:543:299:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:is associated with a larger gain utility as the reference point declines. This latter force does not
25:973:74856:18:18:68:543:281:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:account for much of the results, as we illustrate later when we turn off gain utility. Turning to
25:974:74953:18:18:68:543:263:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:consumption and assets, consumption tracks quite closely the per-period earnings, especially
25:975:75045:18:18:68:543:245:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:in the reference-dependent model. As such, assets get depleted quickly and remain at zero
25:976:75134:18:18:68:543:227:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:or close for the rest of the spell. While we do not observe consumption in our data, other
25:977:75224:18:18:68:543:209:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:work (e.g. Ganong and Noel, 2015) has documented that consumption for UI recipients tracks
25:978:75314:18:18:68:452:191:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:income rather closely with sharp drops at UI entry and benefit exhaustion.
25:979:75388:18:18:85:543:173:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:In the final set of columns in Table 3 we return to a key motivation of the paper. We
25:980:75473:18:18:68:543:155:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:argued that the reference-dependent model can, at least in principle, capture the qualitative
25:981:75566:18:17:68:543:137:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:features of the hazard from unemployment without any heterogeneity. Yet, the estimates of
25:982:75655:17:18:68:543:120:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:reference-dependent model allow for two heterogeneous types. In Columns (5) and (6), we
25:983:75742:18:18:68:543:102:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:remove any heterogeneity and estimate the reference-dependent model with only one cost type.
25:984:75834:18:16:77:543:84:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:F:31The results for the reference-dependent models with either delta or beta-delta discounting are qualitatively
25:985:75944:16:0:68:254:68:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:similar in all the subsequent specifications.
26:986:75991:0:18:68:543:711:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:This bare-bones model fits the data better than the standard model (goodness of fit of 183
26:987:76081:18:18:68:543:693:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:compared to 227), despite having two fewer parameters. As Figure 8d) shows, the qualitative
26:988:76172:18:34:68:466:675:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:fit is almost as good as in the reference-dependent models with heterogeneity.
26:989:76250:34:25:68:285:641:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:C:5.4  Reference-Dependence Variants
26:990:76284:25:18:68:543:616:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:In Table 4 we consider variants of the benchmark reference-dependent model, reproduced in
26:991:76373:18:18:68:543:598:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Column (1). First, instead of defining the reference point as the average of income over the
26:992:76465:18:36:68:441:580:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f5:B:Nprevious periods, we assume an AR(1) process for the reference point:
26:993:76535:36:0:202:363:544:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f14:?:rt=ρrt−1+ (1−ρ)yt= (1−ρ)
26:994:76559:0:25:368:379:557:8:g_d0_f15:10:g_d0_f18:?:∞∑
26:995:76561:25:0:365:379:532:8:g_d0_f12:8:g_d0_f7:?:i=1
26:996:76564:0:37:381:409:544:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f12:?:ρiyt−i
26:997:76570:37:18:85:543:507:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:This updating rule has longer “memory” and adjusts more smoothly than the benchmark
26:998:76653:18:18:68:543:489:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:reference point, with the speed of adjustment captured byρ.32Column (2) of Table 4 shows
26:999:76741:18:18:68:543:471:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the estimated speed of adjustmentρ=0.77, which implies faster adjustment (half-life is 40
26:1000:76830:18:18:68:543:453:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:days) than in the benchmark case. The goodness of fit with AR(1) updating is very similar
26:1001:76919:18:18:68:514:435:12:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f9:B:to the one in the benchmark estimates. Figure 9a) shows the fit of this AR(1) model.33
26:1002:77005:18:17:85:543:417:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Next, we disentangle the role played by gain and loss utility. So far, we have arbitrarily set
26:1003:77099:17:18:68:543:400:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the gain utility parameter,η, to 1 and estimated the weight on loss utility,ηλ. In Columns
26:1004:77189:18:18:68:543:382:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:(3) and (4) we examine the role of gain and loss utility by including only one at a time in the
26:1005:77284:18:18:68:543:364:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:model. In Column (3) we assume no gain utility when workers get a job, but still estimate
26:1006:77373:18:18:68:543:346:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the loss utility weightηλ. The fit of the model, visible in Figure 9b), is almost as good as
26:1007:77465:18:18:68:543:328:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the benchmark reference-dependent one, and the estimated speed of updating of the reference
26:1008:77556:18:18:68:543:310:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:point is nearly the same (though not, as expected, the estimated loss aversion). In Column
26:1009:77646:18:18:68:543:292:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:(4), we instead assume no loss utility and model gain utility. This model does worse than
26:1010:77735:18:18:68:512:274:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:even the standard model (Figure 9c). This indicates the key role played by loss utility.
26:1011:77823:18:18:85:543:256:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Next, we present a parallel take on this result. Columns (5) and (6) report the estimates
26:1012:77912:18:18:68:543:238:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f6:B:setting, respectively, a value ofηof 0.2 and of 5. As the (assumed) weight on gain utilityη
26:1013:78003:18:18:68:543:220:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:increases, the estimatedλdecreases, holding the termηλ, which is the weight on loss utility,
26:1014:78095:18:18:68:543:202:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:at comparable (though not constant) levels.  The goodness of fit under these alternative
26:1015:78183:18:17:68:543:184:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:assumptions forηis almost the same as in the standard model. In Column (7), we estimate
26:1016:78270:17:15:77:543:167:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:F:32When we implement this estimate we assume that the memory of the AR(1) update goes back to 1050
26:1017:78367:15:9:68:190:152:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:days (or 70 15-day periods).
26:1018:78395:9:15:77:543:143:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:F:33We adopt as benchmark the N-period reference point for computational reasons, since the long memory
26:1019:78496:15:12:68:543:128:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:of the AR(1) model makes the estimates more time-consuming. In addition, we do not estimate models with
26:1020:78599:12:12:68:543:116:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:a forward-looking reference point as in Kőszegi and Rabin (2006). These models typically give rise to multiple
26:1021:78709:12:12:68:543:104:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:equilibria which would complicate the model solution significantly. However, notice that it is unclear that
26:1022:78816:12:12:68:543:92:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:forward-looking reference points would explain the difference in hazards after period 270. By that point, the
26:1023:78925:12:12:68:543:80:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:forward-looking reference points would presumably be the same in the pre- and post-period, leading to similar
26:1024:79034:12:0:68:407:68:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:predictions for the two periods (up to equilibrium selection), counterfactually.
27:1025:79116:0:18:68:543:711:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:separatelyηandλ, with the fit shown in Figure 9d). The two parameters are in principle
27:1026:79202:18:18:68:543:693:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:separately identified, but the estimate forη(4.5) is imprecise, and the goodness of fit is again
27:1027:79298:18:34:68:505:675:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:close to the benchmark model. As such, in the rest of the paper we holdηfixed to 1.
27:1028:79381:34:26:68:197:641:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:C:5.5  Habit Formation
27:1029:79401:26:18:68:543:615:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:How does reference dependence compare to habit formation?  Models a la Constantinides
27:1030:79486:18:18:68:543:597:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:(1990) and Campbell and Cochrane (1999) assume utilityu(c−zr),whereris the habit
27:1031:79566:18:18:68:543:579:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:formed from past consumption anduis a concave function. Habit formation, like reference
27:1032:79653:18:18:68:543:561:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:dependence, induces a temporarily high marginal utility following a benefit cut, as consump-
27:1033:79745:18:18:68:533:543:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:tioncgets closer to the habitzr. Thus, it could also plausibly fit the patterns in the data.
27:1034:79837:18:18:85:543:525:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:We estimate a version of a habit formation model replacing the reference dependent utility
27:1035:79927:18:32:68:328:507:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:function (defined in Equation (1)) with the utility:
27:1036:79979:32:33:246:365:475:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:?:v(ct, rt) = log(ct−zrt),
27:1037:80003:33:18:68:543:442:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:wherezcaptures the responsiveness to changes in the habit andrtis calculated the same way
27:1038:80092:18:18:68:543:424:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:as before, but reinterpreted as a measure of habit stock.34The estimates are in Columns (1)
27:1039:80183:18:18:68:543:406:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:and (2) of Table 5, allowing for a fixed N for updating of the reference point and for an AR(1)
27:1040:80278:18:18:68:543:388:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:process. In either case, the fit is not close to the fit of the reference-dependent model, and is
27:1041:80375:18:18:68:433:370:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:in fact slightly worse than the standard model (see also Figures 10a-b).
27:1042:80447:18:18:85:543:352:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:This may appear surprising given the similar intuition behind the two models. The models
27:1043:80535:18:18:68:543:334:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:however differ in a key aspect.  In the reference-dependent model, the impact of the loss,
27:1044:80625:18:18:68:543:316:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f5:B:λ(u(c)−u(r)),on search effort is approximately proportional to the size of the loss. Instead,
27:1045:80718:18:17:68:543:298:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:in the habit-formation model larger decreases in consumption have disproportionate effect,
27:1046:80808:17:18:68:543:281:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:ascgets closer tozr. Given this, the habit-formation model fits the data less well, since it
27:1047:80900:18:18:68:543:263:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:predicts a larger spike at the 90-day (post reform) benefit decrease, and a much smaller spike
27:1048:80994:18:18:68:543:245:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:for the later (proportionally smaller) benefit decreases.35Allowing for three unobserved types
27:1049:81088:18:18:68:297:227:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:(Column (3)) only partially improves the fit.
27:1050:81133:18:18:85:543:209:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Next, we consider a second type of habit-formation models with so-called multiplicative
27:1051:81220:18:18:68:543:191:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:habits (Abel, 1990), with utilitylog(c)−zlog(r).This utility function is equivalent (up to a
27:1052:81312:18:18:68:543:173:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:linear transformation) to our reference-dependent model with loss aversionλset to 1 andηset
27:1053:81403:18:18:68:543:155:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:toz/(1−z). In Column (4) (and in Figure 10d) we present estimates from this multiplicative-
27:1054:81494:18:18:68:543:137:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:habit model: the fit is superior to the one of the first habit-formation model, though still not
27:1055:81590:18:15:77:543:119:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:F:34Observe that this function is not defined wheneverct< zrt, complicating the estimation. To avoid this
27:1056:81693:15:12:68:543:104:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:problem, Campbell and Cochrane (1999) madeza non-linear function ofyt−rt. We treatzas a parameter
27:1057:81790:12:9:68:484:92:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:instead and check in the optimum that our utility function is defined for the relevantytandrt.
27:1058:81884:9:15:77:543:83:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:F:35In addition, the habit-formation model is also computationally trickier to estimate, as the estimated habit
27:1059:81993:15:0:68:309:68:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:parameterγhas to always satisfy the conditionc > γr.
28:1060:82047:0:35:68:524:711:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:close to the benchmark model. This result stresses again the importance of loss aversion.
28:1061:82136:35:25:68:165:676:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:C:5.6  Robustness
28:1062:82151:25:18:68:543:651:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:In Table 6 we consider the robustness of the standard and reference-dependent model to
28:1063:82237:18:18:68:476:633:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:alternative specifications. We present additional checks in Appendix Table A-1.
28:1064:82316:18:18:85:544:615:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:In Column (1) we estimate both time preference parameters,βandδ. While the fit is
28:1065:82397:18:18:68:543:597:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:somewhat better than in the benchmark models, the two time preference parameters appear
28:1066:82484:18:18:68:543:579:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:to be quite collinear; thus, in the benchmark specifications we fix one of the two parameters.
28:1067:82578:18:18:68:543:561:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:In Column (2) we allow for background consumption: workers receive non-market income
28:1068:82662:18:18:68:543:543:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f5:B:nduring unemployment, in addition to the benefits earned, to capture home production.
28:1069:82747:18:18:68:543:525:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:The estimates are very similar to the standard ones. In Column (3), while still allowing for
28:1070:82839:18:18:68:543:507:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:background consumption, we make the alternative assumption that workers are not eligible
28:1071:82927:18:17:68:544:489:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:for welfare; thus, benefits fall to 0 after 360 days.36This alternative assumption improves
28:1072:83018:17:18:68:543:472:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:somewhat the fit of both the reference-dependent model and the standard model. In Column
28:1073:83106:18:18:68:543:454:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:(4) we allow for a linear time trend in the baseline cost factor allowing for skill depreciation or
28:1074:83205:18:18:68:543:436:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:conversely learning by searching. This additional parameter improves the fit of the reference-
28:1075:83299:18:18:68:493:418:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:dependent model while leaving the fit of the standard model essentially unaffected.
28:1076:83382:18:18:85:543:400:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:In Column (5) we explore the role played by the spikes at days 270 and 360, since one may
28:1077:83471:18:18:68:543:382:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:worry that such spikes play a disproportional role in the identification given the quadratic
28:1078:83563:18:18:68:543:364:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:distance measure. The model estimated dropping these moments from the objective function
28:1079:83651:18:18:68:543:346:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:(see also Appendix Figure A-7c and d) yields similar patterns indicating that the results are
28:1080:83744:18:18:68:543:328:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:not driven by the spikes. We also consider the importance of timing in the model. We assume
28:1081:83835:18:18:68:543:310:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:that jobs start one period after the offer is received, but what if the hiring process takes longer?
28:1082:83935:18:18:68:543:292:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:In Column (6) we assume a 2-period (that is, approximately one-month) delay between the
28:1083:84022:18:18:68:543:274:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:job search and the start of the wage payments. As Appendix Figures A-8a) and b) show, the
28:1084:84111:18:17:68:543:256:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:change in timing does not shift the spike. Rather, job-seekers start their search effort earlier,
28:1085:84208:17:18:68:543:239:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:taking into account the longer delay. In Column (7) we allow for a lower reemployment wage
28:1086:84298:18:18:68:543:221:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:set at two thirds of the average previous wage. The alternative assumption worsens somewhat
28:1087:84389:18:18:68:543:203:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the fit of both models, with little substantive impact on the estimates. In Column (8) we test
28:1088:84483:18:18:68:543:185:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the importance of the assumption of zero initial assets: endowing workers with $600 in their
28:1089:84575:18:18:68:543:167:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:last period of employment does not affect much the estimates (see also Appendix Figure A-7a)
28:1090:84667:18:18:68:543:149:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:and b)), though the fit is somewhat worse than under the assumption of zero assets (which
28:1091:84756:18:18:68:381:131:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:itself is most consistent with the estimated high impatience).
28:1092:84818:18:18:85:543:113:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:In Appendix Table A-1 we present additional robustness results. In Column (1) we use
28:1093:84902:18:16:77:543:95:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:F:36While unemployed workers are generally eligible for welfare after benefit exhaustion, the rules are complex
28:1094:85011:16:0:68:471:79:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:and we do not observe a good measure of welfare take up. Thus, we explore this alternative.
29:1095:85104:0:18:68:543:711:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the identity matrix to weight the moments and in Column (2) we use the moments estimated
29:1096:85192:18:18:68:543:693:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:after controlling for observables (shown in Figure 5b)). Though the goodness of fit cannot be
29:1097:85285:18:18:68:543:675:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:compared to the previous estimates, the qualitative conclusions remain the same. In Columns
29:1098:85376:18:18:68:543:657:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:(3) and (4), instead of using the hazard rates as moments, we use the estimated (unconditional)
29:1099:85471:18:17:68:543:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:probability of exiting unemployment in each 15-day period. The advantage of this alternative
29:1100:85563:17:18:68:543:622:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:procedure is that we can use the full variance-covariance matrix for weights, which we do
29:1101:85652:18:18:68:543:604:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:in Column (3). Once again, while the goodness of fit measures are not comparable to the
29:1102:85739:18:18:68:543:586:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:benchmark models, the pattern of the results is very similar. Finally, the estimates are similar
29:1103:85835:18:35:68:443:568:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:if we use the 30-day hazards or 7-day hazards, instead of 15-day hazards.
29:1104:85908:35:26:68:255:533:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:C:5.7  Unobserved Heterogeneity
29:1105:85937:26:18:68:543:507:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:So far, we have modeled one form of heterogeneity, in search costs,37and allowed for a fixed
29:1106:86029:18:17:68:543:489:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:number of types: three in the standard model and two in the behavioral model. In Table 7,
29:1107:86118:17:18:68:208:472:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:we relax both assumptions.
29:1108:86144:18:18:85:543:454:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:First, we increase the number of heterogeneous cost types from 2 types (Column (1)) all the
29:1109:86235:18:18:68:543:436:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:way to 5 types (Column (4)). Allowing for additional types in the standard model improves the
29:1110:86328:18:18:68:543:418:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:fit all the way to 4 types; estimates with 5 or more types have trouble converging. Still, even
29:1111:86423:18:18:68:543:400:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the model with 4 or 5 types does significantly worse in terms of fit than the reference-dependent
29:1112:86520:18:18:68:543:382:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:model (Figure 11a). For the reference-dependent model, there is essentially no improvement
29:1113:86610:18:18:68:543:364:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:in fit going from 2 types to more types. Indeed, estimates of the reference-dependent model
29:1114:86701:18:18:68:317:346:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:with more than 3 types have trouble converging.
29:1115:86748:18:18:85:543:328:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Next, we consider alternative forms of unobserved heterogeneity, such as in the reemploy-
29:1116:86837:18:18:68:543:310:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:ment wage. We take the 10th, 50th, and 90th percentile of the reemployment wage, as well
29:1117:86925:18:18:68:543:292:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:as the fractions of each type (taken to be 20 percent, 60 percent, and 20 percent respectively)
29:1118:87020:18:18:68:543:274:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:from the data. We then estimate three cost parameterskj, one for each type. This alternative
29:1119:87112:18:17:68:543:256:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:specification (Column (5)) improves somewhat the fit of the standard model, as Figure 11b)
29:1120:87202:17:18:68:493:239:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:also shows, but the fit of the reference-dependent model is still significantly better.
29:1121:87289:18:18:85:544:221:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:In Column (6) we allow for heterogeneity in the curvature parameterγ. While this form
29:1122:87374:18:18:68:543:203:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:of heterogeneity does not affect the estimates of the reference-dependent model, it improves
29:1123:87466:18:18:68:543:185:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:dramatically the fit of the standard model. The estimates fit both the spike at 270 and at 360
29:1124:87560:18:18:68:543:167:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:days, as well as the difference between the pre- and post-reform period (Figure 11(c)). Indeed,
29:1125:87655:18:18:68:543:149:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:this is the only specification with a better fit than the reference-dependent model. How does
29:1126:87748:18:18:68:543:131:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:this model attain such good fit? The fit relies heavily on types with a very different elasticity
29:1127:87845:18:18:68:543:113:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:of search (which equals1/γ). Initially, most exits are of the low-elasticity types (γhigh= 1.01),
29:1128:87942:18:18:68:543:95:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:but the spike at day 270 is driven by the medium-elasticity types (γmed= 0.20). These types
29:1129:88033:18:0:77:522:77:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:A:37The heterogeneity on search costs is based on papers such as Bloemen (2005) and Paserman (2008).
30:1130:88133:0:18:68:543:711:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:were barely searching up until day 270, but their search intensity spikes in presence of lower
30:1131:88227:18:18:68:543:693:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:benefits. The spike at 360 days is due to the high-elasticity types, with an elasticity over 50
30:1132:88322:18:18:68:424:675:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:(γlow= 0.016), that start searching once benefits hit the welfare level.
30:1133:88394:18:18:85:543:657:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:How critical are the high-elasticity types for the fit of the model?  We re-estimate the
30:1134:88482:18:17:68:543:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:model restricting the elasticity to be no higher than 10 (γ≥0.1, Column (7)) and no higher
30:1135:88572:17:18:68:543:622:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:than 5 (γ≥0.2, Column (8)). With such restrictions in place, the model does not match any
30:1136:88661:18:35:68:421:604:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:more the fit of the reference-dependent model (see also Figure 11d)).
30:1137:88730:35:26:68:281:569:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:C:5.8  Tests of Gamma Heterogeneity
30:1138:88763:26:18:68:543:543:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:The previous sections show that only one version of the standard model matches (in fact,
30:1139:88851:18:17:68:543:525:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:surpasses) the fit of the reference-dependent model: the gamma-heterogeneity model. How
30:1140:88938:17:18:68:543:508:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:should we compare then the two models? In this section we present a calibration and additional
30:1141:89032:18:18:68:320:490:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:evidence, with more evidence in the next section.
30:1142:89081:18:18:85:543:472:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:As we just showed, the gamma-heterogeneity model requires two types with a very large
30:1143:89166:18:18:68:543:454:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:elasticity of search effort. To consider the implications of such elasticities, consider a hypo-
30:1144:89262:18:18:68:543:436:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:thetical comparative statics: a 10 percent increase, or decrease, in the level of welfare benefits
30:1145:89360:18:18:68:543:418:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:paid out after 360 days. Figures 12a) and b) show that, in response to such a small benefit
30:1146:89451:18:18:68:543:400:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:change, the search effort of reference-dependent workers would barely change compared to the
30:1147:89543:18:18:68:543:382:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:benchmark estimates. In the gamma-heterogeneity model, instead, the hazard rates would
30:1148:89629:18:18:68:543:364:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:go to nearly 1 (for a 10% benefit decrease, Figure 12a)) or to nearly 0 (for a 10% increase,
30:1149:89721:18:18:68:543:346:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Figure 12b)). The workers still unemployed by day 360 are mostly the high-elasticity types,
30:1150:89812:18:18:68:543:328:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:and even small changes in the setting lead to massive changes in search effort. We find this
30:1151:89904:18:18:68:543:310:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:implied response unrealistic. Importantly, this high elasticity, and thus the ensuing extreme
30:1152:89997:18:18:68:514:292:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:response, is critical for the fit of the gamma-elasticity model, as we documented above.
30:1153:90085:18:17:85:543:274:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:While there are no reforms in the welfare system in our period, we do observe a reform
30:1154:90171:17:18:68:543:257:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:in the unemployment assistance system occurring one year before our ‘pre’ period (see Figure
30:1155:90263:18:18:68:543:239:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:3). The benefit duration in this second tier goes from 180 days before the change to 90 days
30:1156:90355:18:18:68:543:221:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:after the change for the individuals in our sample.38We use this reform, which we have not
30:1157:90445:18:18:68:543:203:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:employed so far, as an out-of-sample test, since the macroeconomic setting was similar and
30:1158:90535:18:18:68:281:185:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the group of workers affected is the same.
30:1159:90577:18:18:85:543:167:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Figure 12c) shows the hazard for the 2-year-prior period, as well as the predictions for
30:1160:90665:18:18:68:543:149:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the benchmark reference-dependent estimates (Column (4) in Table 3) and for the gamma-
30:1161:90751:18:18:68:543:131:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:heterogeneity estimates (Column (6) in Table 7).  The reference-dependent model fits the
30:1162:90839:18:18:68:543:113:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:pre-period well, with an out-of-sample SSE of 73.4. The gamma heterogeneity model, instead,
30:1163:90930:18:15:77:543:95:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:F:38This change applied to individuals aged 45 and lower. Since our sample includes only individuals up to 50
30:1164:91037:15:12:68:543:80:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:years old, the reform applies to the large majority of the sample. Excluding the 46-50 year olds barely affects
30:1165:91148:12:0:68:128:68:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:the estimates.
31:1166:91164:0:18:68:543:711:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:fits quite poorly the period of the lengthened unemployment assistance (between 300 and
31:1167:91251:18:18:68:543:693:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:450 days), with an out-of-sample SSE of 130.5. In this period, the medium-elasticity types
31:1168:91341:18:18:68:543:675:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:are disappearing from the sample, and the high-elasticity types are not searching yet. The
31:1169:91431:18:18:68:543:657:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:gamma heterogeneity type appears to overfit the data in sample. The out-of-sample fit of the
31:1170:91523:18:17:68:543:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:standard model with heterogeneity in cost levels is better (SSE of 91.6), but does not reach
31:1171:91615:17:35:68:518:622:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the reference-dependent model. We present a final piece of evidence in the next section.
31:1172:91703:35:26:68:353:587:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:C:5.9  Dynamic Selection throughout the UI spell
31:1173:91749:26:18:68:543:561:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:The standard model captures reasonably well the dynamics in the hazards in the first 270
31:1174:91837:18:18:68:543:543:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:days as well as some of the trend after that, especially in the gamma-heterogeneity version.
31:1175:91929:18:18:68:543:525:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:To achieve this fit, changes in the unobserved types over time play a key role; without types,
31:1176:92023:18:18:68:543:507:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the hazards would be monotonically (weakly) increasing over time. How plausible then is the
31:1177:92114:18:17:68:543:489:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:amount of heterogeneity that the standard model requires? And how does it compare to the
31:1178:92202:17:18:68:355:472:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:heterogeneity needed in the reference-dependent model?
31:1179:92256:18:18:85:543:454:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:While we cannot measure the time-changing unobserved heterogeneity, we propose that a
31:1180:92341:18:18:68:543:436:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:useful metric is the time-varying selection on observables of the unemployed. Assuming that
31:1181:92432:18:18:68:543:418:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:unobservable factors that influence job search correlate with these observable characteristics,
31:1182:92527:18:18:68:284:400:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:these estimates convey useful information.
31:1183:92569:18:18:85:543:382:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:To document the dynamic selection along observables, we regress at the individual level
31:1184:92656:18:18:68:543:364:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the realized unemployment duration (censored at 540 days) on a rich set of observables:
31:1185:92743:18:18:68:543:346:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:education, age groups, gender, waiting period (the number of days between job lost and UI
31:1186:92832:18:18:68:543:328:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:claimed), log past earnings, indicators for county of residence, day of the month UI claimed,
31:1187:92925:18:18:68:543:310:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:and occupation (1 digit) of the last job.  As Web Appendix Table WA-1 shows, for both
31:1188:93010:18:18:68:543:292:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:individuals in the pre- and post-sample (Columns (1) and (2)), these variables are reliable
31:1189:93101:18:18:68:543:274:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:predictors of non-employment duration, with anR2of 0.05-0.06.  Panel B shows that the
31:1190:93186:18:17:68:543:256:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:predicted unemployment duration based on these estimates for the pre-period varies between
31:1191:93276:17:18:68:505:239:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:230 days (5th percentile) and 370 days (95th percentile), a good amount of variation.
31:1192:93361:18:18:85:543:221:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:The dotted lines with crosses in Figure 13a-b) show the predicted unemployment duration
31:1193:93448:18:18:68:543:203:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:averaging across all individuals who exit unemployment in a given 15-day period. While pre-
31:1194:93539:18:18:68:543:185:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:dicted unemployment increases (unsurprisingly) throughout the spell, the overall relationship
31:1195:93632:18:18:68:543:167:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:is quite flat: predicted unemployment only increases from 280 days to 295 days after around 2
31:1196:93725:18:18:68:543:149:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:years. Furthermore, the pattern of selection is not much affected by the UI benefit path. The
31:1197:93818:18:18:68:544:131:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:lines for the pre- and post-reform period are fairly parallel for most of the spell.39Selection
31:1198:93913:18:18:68:359:113:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:on observables thus plays only a limited role in the data.
31:1199:93971:18:15:77:543:95:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:F:39The fact that dynamic selection seems to be small and not much affected by the UI regime has been shown
31:1200:94076:15:0:68:259:80:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:before, for example Schmieder et al. (2016).
32:1201:94122:0:18:85:543:711:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:The selection over time in predicted duration has a counterpart in the structural models.
32:1202:94211:18:18:68:543:693:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:For each cost type, we compute the expected unemployment duration in the pre-reform period.
32:1203:94302:18:18:68:543:675:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:We then calculate the average expected duration for unemployed individuals who leave in a
32:1204:94391:18:18:68:543:657:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:given period according to the estimated models. The reference-dependent model (solid lines
32:1205:94481:18:17:68:543:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:in Figure 13) predicts that the type composition changes only in the first 90 days and not
32:1206:94571:17:18:68:543:622:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:by much; thus the expected unemployment duration line is quite flat and not so dissimilar
32:1207:94660:18:18:68:543:604:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:from what we observe empirically. The standard model with cost heterogeneity (Figure 13a))
32:1208:94750:18:18:68:543:586:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:instead displays a large amount of dynamic selection, with a large swing until 200 days, with no
32:1209:94846:18:18:68:543:568:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:corresponding evidence in the data. The predicted heterogeneity for the gamma-heterogeneity
32:1210:94937:18:18:68:543:550:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:model (Figure 13b)) is even more at odds with the data, with an initial swing, and then an
32:1211:95027:18:18:68:543:532:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:abrupt swing between 300 and 360 days, corresponding to the transition from the medium-
32:1212:95114:18:18:68:502:514:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:to the high-elasticity type. There is no evidence of a corresponding shift in the data.
32:1213:95201:18:18:85:543:496:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Thus, the pattern of dynamic selection implied by two versions of the standard model
32:1214:95285:18:18:68:543:478:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:appear at odds with the much more muted and monotonic selection in the data. We should
32:1215:95371:18:18:68:543:460:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:be clear that it is not surprising that the selection on observables be more muted than the
32:1216:95462:18:18:68:543:442:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:selection implied by the model, given that we only observe part of the selection. However,
32:1217:95552:18:18:68:543:424:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the extent of the difference is quite striking, given the rich set of variables used in calculating
32:1218:95651:18:18:68:543:406:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:dynamic selection. Moreover, it is puzzling for the standard model that the observed selection
32:1219:95745:18:34:68:531:388:12:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f9:B:does not display any of the trends in the model predictions, even on a more muted scale.40
32:1220:95835:34:26:68:216:354:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:C:5.10  Reservation Wages
32:1221:95858:26:18:68:542:328:12:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f9:B:So far we take the reemployment wage as fixed so the unemployed accept every job offer.41
32:1222:95947:18:18:68:543:310:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:While this is consistent with a literature documenting a small role of reservation wages for job
32:1223:96043:18:18:68:543:292:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:search dynamics (e.g. Card et al., 2007a; Krueger and Mueller, 2013; Schmieder et al., 2016),
32:1224:96136:18:17:68:543:274:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:a natural question is whether introducing a reservation wage would change our conclusions.
32:1225:96226:17:18:68:543:257:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:For tractability, we estimate a reference-dependent model with choice of search effort and
32:1226:96316:18:18:68:543:239:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:reservation wage, but with hand-to-mouth consumers.  These results should be considered
32:1227:96403:18:18:68:505:221:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:only suggestive, as endogenizing consumption is important, as we documented above.
32:1228:96485:18:18:85:543:203:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:We reestimate the model incorporating job acceptance decisions, using additional moments
32:1229:96573:18:18:68:543:185:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:based on reemployment wages and solving by backward induction. In this expanded model,
32:1230:96659:18:15:77:543:167:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:F:40While the Altonji et al. (2005) procedure motivates this test, the parallel is imperfect.  Altonji et al.
32:1231:96766:15:12:68:543:152:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:(2005) infers the impact of unobservable variables on the the magnitude of an estimate, based on the impact
32:1232:96873:12:12:68:543:140:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:of the observables. In our case, we would like to infer the extent and the role of unobserved heterogeneity
32:1233:96980:12:12:68:543:128:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:in the structural model, based on the heterogeneity captured by observables. The underlying assumption of
32:1234:97085:12:12:68:543:116:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:our procedure is that the set of observable variables used in predicting dynamic selections are rich enough to
32:1235:97195:12:12:68:543:104:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:capture (part of) the dynamic selection if its there, and also that the time path of dynamic selection caused
32:1236:97304:12:9:68:362:92:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:by observables is similar to the one caused by unobserved factors. .
32:1237:97372:9:15:77:543:83:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:F:41In the baseline model there is a single reemployment wage for everyone, while in the models in Table 7,
32:1238:97477:15:0:68:519:68:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:Column (5) we allow for 3 different groups of workers, each facing a different fixed reemployment wage.
33:1239:97582:0:18:68:543:711:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:individuals draw job offers from a (stationary) log-normal wage offer distribution and decide
33:1240:97675:18:18:68:543:693:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:whether or not to accept it. For simplicity, we also abstract away from gain (or loss) utility
33:1241:97769:18:18:68:543:675:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:at reemployment. We set the standard deviation of the wage offer distribution at 0.5, close
33:1242:97860:18:18:68:543:657:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:to the standard deviation of the actual reemployment wages, and we estimate the mean of
33:1243:97947:18:17:68:543:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the wage offer distribution. As additional moments, we use the average reemployment wage
33:1244:98035:17:18:68:544:622:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:of individuals exiting unemployment in periodtafter entering the UI system, adding 70
33:1245:98120:18:18:68:364:604:12:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f9:B:additional moments in the minimum distance estimator.42
33:1246:98175:18:18:85:543:586:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:Web Appendix Table WA-2 shows the results in Columns (1) and (2).  The reference-
33:1247:98256:18:18:68:543:568:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:dependent model has a better fit than the standard model (GOF of 272 versus 308), largely
33:1248:98345:18:18:68:543:550:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:due to the reference-dependent model providing a better fit for the hazard moments (see also
33:1249:98437:18:18:68:543:532:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:Web Appendix Figures WA-1). Notice however that the estimates in Columns (1) and (2), not
33:1250:98526:18:18:68:543:514:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:unlike the hand-to-mouth estimates in Table 2, have relatively patient unemployed workers,
33:1251:98616:18:18:68:543:496:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:at odds with the maintained assumption of hand-to-mouth consumption.  For the sake of
33:1252:98701:18:18:68:543:478:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:comparison, Columns (3) and (4) provide estimates of the hand-to-mouth models with no
33:1253:98786:18:36:68:408:460:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:reservation wage choice assuming no gain utility at reemployment.
33:1254:98851:36:30:68:244:424:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:C:6  Discussion and Conclusion
33:1255:98879:30:17:68:543:394:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:We provided evidence that a model with reference-dependent preferences can explain qual-
33:1256:98967:17:18:68:543:377:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:itative features of the hazards which plausible versions of the standard model have a hard
33:1257:99057:18:18:68:543:359:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:time fitting. The model itself builds on one of the most robust behavioral models, reference
33:1258:99149:18:18:68:543:341:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:dependence, and uses a natural candidate for a backward-looking reference point. We also
33:1259:99237:18:18:68:543:323:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:find that job seekers are substantially impatient, likely in the form of present-bias preferences.
33:1260:99335:18:18:85:543:305:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A:This evidence has policy implications. A key prediction of reference-dependent job search
33:1261:99424:18:18:68:543:287:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:is that step-wise benefit schedule can induce considerably more search than predicted by
33:1262:99512:18:18:68:543:269:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the standard job search models.  Moreover, if the social planner wants the job seekers to
33:1263:99601:18:18:68:543:251:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:search more than they do (e.g. because she uses a lower discount rate for welfare assessment
33:1264:99693:18:18:68:543:233:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:than that of the unemployed), then having benefit drops over the UI spell is likely to be an
33:1265:99785:18:18:68:543:215:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:inexpensive way to achieve that goal. In support of that, Lindner and Reizer (2015) show that
33:1266:99878:18:18:68:543:197:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:the Hungarian UI reform examined here did not just speed up exits to employment, but it was
33:1267:99969:18:18:68:543:179:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:revenue-neutral from the perspective of the government. Therefore, the evidence presented
33:1268:100058:18:17:68:473:161:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:B:here opens the door to potential redesigns in unemployment insurance policies.
33:1269:100136:17:16:77:543:144:7:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f11:F:42The reemployment wage moments are estimated as in Lindner and Reizer (2015), allowing for a linear
33:1270:100236:16:0:68:117:128:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:F:time trend.

47:1608:120356:12:12:90:522:332:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:?:that we use for our before-after comparison. The timing of the reform was the following: those
47:1609:120450:12:12:90:522:320:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:?:who claimed UI benefit before February 5th, 2005 faced the old first tier schedule and old second
47:1610:120547:12:12:90:522:308:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:?:tier schedule; those who claimed benefit between February 5th, 2005 and October 31th, 2005 faced
47:1611:120643:12:12:90:522:296:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:?:the old benefit schedule in the first tier and the new benefit schedule in the second tier; those who
47:1612:120744:12:12:90:522:284:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:?:claimed benefit after November 1st, 2005 faced the new benefit schedule in the first tier and the new
47:1613:120845:12:12:90:522:272:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:?:benefit schedule in the second tier. To avoid complications caused by changes in the second tier, in
47:1614:120945:12:12:90:522:260:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:?:our main specifications we focus on the (1 year) before sample, i.e. those who claimed UI between
47:1615:121042:12:12:90:522:248:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:?:February 5th, 2005 and October 15th, 2005, and (1 year) after sample, i.e. those who claimed UI
47:1616:121137:12:12:90:522:236:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:?:between February 5th, 2006 and October 15th, 2006. We use the (2 year) before sample and the (2
47:1617:121232:12:12:90:522:224:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:?:year) after sample to show that the changes in the hazard rates are in line with the timing of the
47:1618:121330:12:12:90:522:212:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:?:reform. The first tier changes before and after October 31th, 2005 are presented in Figure 2. The
47:1619:121427:12:12:90:522:200:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:?:changes in the second tier in February 5th, 2005 were the following: potential duration shortened to
47:1620:121527:12:12:90:522:188:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:?:180 days above age 50 and to 90 days below that. Before, it was 270 days above age 45 and 180 days
47:1621:121625:12:0:90:511:176:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:?:below that. The benefit level was raised slightly from 21,000 HUF ($101) to 22,800 HUF ($114).
